<tr>
<td class="noprint" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff99cc">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_berkelium" title="Template:Infobox berkelium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_berkelium" title="Template talk:Infobox berkelium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_berkelium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
| <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a> | <a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q1882" title="d:Q1882">in Wikidata</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Berkelium</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic</a> <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Bk</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 97. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranium element</a> series. It is named after the city of <a href="/wiki/Berkeley,_California" title="Berkeley, California">Berkeley, California</a>, the location of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_California_Radiation_Laboratory" title="University of California Radiation Laboratory">University of California Radiation Laboratory</a> where it was discovered in December 1949. This was the fifth transuranium element discovered after <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>.</p>
<p>The major <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of berkelium, <sup>249</sup>Bk, is synthesized in minute quantities in dedicated high-flux <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, mainly at the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, USA, and at the <a href="/wiki/Research_Institute_of_Atomic_Reactors" title="Research Institute of Atomic Reactors">Research Institute of Atomic Reactors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dimitrovgrad,_Russia" title="Dimitrovgrad, Russia">Dimitrovgrad, Russia</a>. The production of the second-most important isotope <sup>247</sup>Bk involves the irradiation of the rare isotope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curium-244" title="Curium-244"><sup>244</sup>Cm</a> with high-energy <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>.</p>
<p>Just over one gram of berkelium has been produced in the United States since 1967. There is no practical application of berkelium outside of scientific research which is mostly directed at the synthesis of heavier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_elements" title="Transuranic elements">transuranic elements</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transactinide" title="Transactinide">transactinides</a>. A 22 milligram batch of berkelium-249 was prepared during a 250-day irradiation period and then purified for a further 90 days at Oak Ridge in 2009. This sample was used to synthesize the element <a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">tennessine</a> for the first time in 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Joint Institute for Nuclear Research</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, after it was bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Calcium-48" title="Calcium-48">calcium-48</a> ions for 150 days. This was the culmination of the Russia–US collaboration on the synthesis of elements 113 to 118.</p>
<p>Berkelium is a soft, silvery-white, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a> metal. The berkelium-249 isotope emits low-energy <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and thus is relatively safe to handle. It decays with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 330 days to <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a>-249, which is a strong emitter of ionizing alpha particles. This gradual transformation is an important consideration when studying the properties of elemental berkelium and its chemical compounds, since the formation of californium brings not only chemical contamination, but also free-radical effects and self-heating from the emitted helium nuclei.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Closest_packing_ABAC.png"><img alt="Sequential layers of spheres arranged from top to bottom: GRGBGRGB (G=green, R=red, B=blue)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1299" data-file-width="1417" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/220px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/330px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Closest_packing_ABAC.png/440px-Closest_packing_ABAC.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Closest_packing_ABAC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Double-hexagonal close packing with the layer sequence ABAC in the crystal structure of α-berkelium (A: green, B: blue, C: red)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Berkelium is a soft, silvery-white, radioactive <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> metal. In the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, it is located to the right of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>, to the left of the actinide <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a> and below the lanthanide <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a> with which it shares many similarities in physical and chemical properties. Its density of 14.78 g/cm<sup>3</sup> lies between those of curium (13.52 g/cm<sup>3</sup>) and californium (15.1 g/cm<sup>3</sup>), as does its melting point of 986 °C, below that of curium (1340 °C) but higher than that of californium (900 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-2">[2]</a></sup> Berkelium is relatively soft and has one of the lowest <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk moduli</a> among the actinides, at about 20 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a> (2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup> Pa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pressure-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Berkelium(III) ions shows two sharp <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a> peaks at 652 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanometer" title="Nanometer">nanometers</a> (red light) and 742 nanometers (deep red – near infrared) due to internal transitions at the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">f-electron shell</a>. The relative intensity of these peaks depends on the excitation power and temperature of the sample. This emission can be observed, for example, after dispersing berkelium ions in a silicate glass, by melting the glass in presence of berkelium oxide or halide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 70 K and room temperature, berkelium behaves as a <a href="/wiki/Curie%E2%80%93Weiss_law" title="Curie–Weiss law">Curie–Weiss</a> paramagnetic material with an effective magnetic moment of 9.69 <a href="/wiki/Bohr_magneton" title="Bohr magneton">Bohr magnetons</a> (µ<sub>B</sub>) and a <a href="/wiki/Curie_temperature" title="Curie temperature">Curie temperature</a> of 101 K. This magnetic moment is almost equal to the theoretical value of 9.72 µ<sub>B</sub> calculated within the simple atomic <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum_coupling" title="Angular momentum coupling">L-S coupling model</a>. Upon cooling to about 34 K, berkelium undergoes a transition to an <a href="/wiki/Antiferromagnetism" title="Antiferromagnetism">antiferromagnetic</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198445_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198445-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_change_of_solution" title="Enthalpy change of solution">Enthalpy of dissolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> at standard conditions is −600 kJ/mol<sup>−1</sup>, from which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_change_of_formation_(data_table)" title="Standard enthalpy change of formation (data table)">standard enthalpy change of formation</a> (Δ<sub>f</sub><i>H</i>°) of aqueous Bk<sup>3+</sup> ions is obtained as −601 kJ/mol<sup>−1</sup>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_potential" title="Standard potential">standard potential</a> Bk<sup>3+</sup>/Bk<sup>0</sup> is −2.01 V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization potential</a> of a neutral berkelium atom is 6.23 eV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198434_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198434-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allotropes">Allotropes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Allotropes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At ambient conditions, berkelium assumes its most stable α form which has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal</a> symmetry, <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> <i>P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc</i>, lattice parameters of 341 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a> and 1107 pm. The crystal has a double-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close-packing_of_spheres" title="Close-packing of spheres">hexagonal close packing</a> structure with the layer sequence ABAC and so is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotypic" title="Isotypic">isotypic</a> (having a similar structure) with α-lanthanum and α-forms of actinides beyond curium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-9">[9]</a></sup> This crystal structure changes with pressure and temperature. When compressed at room temperature to 7 GPa, α-berkelium transforms to the beta modification, which has a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic</a> (<i>fcc</i>) symmetry and space group <i>Fm<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>m</i>. This transition occurs without change in volume, but the <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> increases by 3.66 kJ/mol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198444_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198444-10">[10]</a></sup> Upon further compression to 25 GPa, berkelium transforms to an <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">orthorhombic</a> γ-berkelium structure similar to that of α-uranium. This transition is accompanied by a 12% volume decrease and delocalization of the electrons at the <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">5f electron shell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pressure2-11">[11]</a></sup> No further phase transitions are observed up to 57 GPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pressure-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon heating, α-berkelium transforms into another phase with an <i>fcc</i> lattice (but slightly different from β-berkelium), space group <i>Fm<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>m</i> and the lattice constant of 500 pm; this <i>fcc</i> structure is equivalent to the closest packing with the sequence ABC. This phase is metastable and will gradually revert to the original α-berkelium phase at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-9">[9]</a></sup> The temperature of the phase transition is believed to be quite close to the melting point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahey_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fahey-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like all <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>, berkelium dissolves in various aqueous inorganic acids, liberating gaseous <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and converting into the berkelium(III) state. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trivalent" title="Trivalent">trivalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> (+3) is the most stable, especially in aqueous solutions, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetravalent" title="Tetravalent">tetravalent</a> (+4) and possibly <a href="/wiki/Divalent" title="Divalent">divalent</a> (+2) berkelium compounds are also known. The existence of divalent berkelium salts is uncertain and has only been reported in mixed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthanum_chloride" title="Lanthanum chloride">lanthanum chloride</a>-<a href="/wiki/Strontium_chloride" title="Strontium chloride">strontium chloride</a> melts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198455_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198455-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> A similar behavior is observed for the lanthanide analogue of berkelium, <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-c1-18">[18]</a></sup> Aqueous solutions of Bk<sup>3+</sup> ions are green in most acids. The color of Bk<sup>4+</sup> ions is yellow in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and orange-yellow in <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198455_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198455-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071956_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071956-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971265_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971265-20">[20]</a></sup> Berkelium does not react rapidly with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> at room temperature, possibly due to the formation of a protective oxide layer surface. However, it reacts with molten metals, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogens</a> to form various binary compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198445_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198445-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_berkelium" title="Isotopes of berkelium">Isotopes of berkelium</a></div>
<p>About twenty isotopes and six <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a> (excited states of an isotope) of berkelium have been characterized with the mass numbers ranging from 235 to 254. All of them are radioactive. The longest <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> are observed for <sup>247</sup>Bk (1,380 years), <sup>248</sup>Bk (over 300 years) and <sup>249</sup>Bk (330 days); the half-lives of the other isotopes range from microseconds to several days. The isotope which is the easiest to synthesize is berkelium-249. This emits mostly soft <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β-particles</a> which are inconvenient for detection. Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_radiation" title="Alpha radiation">alpha radiation</a> is rather weak – 1.45<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−<span style="white-space:nowrap">3</span></sup>% with respect to the β-radiation – but is sometimes used to detect this isotope. The second important berkelium isotope, berkelium-247, is an alpha-emitter, as are most actinide isotopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TBE_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-TBE-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All berkelium isotopes have a half-life far too short to be <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a>. Therefore, any primordial berkelium, that is, berkelium present on the Earth during its formation, has decayed by now.</p>
<p>On Earth, berkelium is mostly concentrated in certain areas, which were used for the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons tests</a> between 1945 and 1980, as well as at the sites of nuclear incidents, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> and <a href="/wiki/1968_Thule_Air_Base_B-52_crash" title="1968 Thule Air Base B-52 crash">1968 Thule Air Base B-52 crash</a>. Analysis of the debris at the testing site of the first U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a>, (1 November 1952, <a href="/wiki/Enewetak_Atoll" title="Enewetak Atoll">Enewetak Atoll</a>), revealed high concentrations of various actinides, including berkelium. For reasons of military secrecy, this result was published only in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nuclear reactors produce mostly, among the berkelium isotopes, berkelium-249. During the storage and before the fuel disposal, most of it <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decays</a> to californium-249. The latter has a half-life of 351 years, which is relatively long when compared to the other isotopes produced in the reactor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and is therefore undesirable in the disposal products.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">transuranic elements</a> from <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fermium" title="Fermium">fermium</a>, including berkelium, occurred naturally in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, but no longer do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2426" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg/170px-Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg/255px-Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg/340px-Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glenn_Seaborg_-_1964.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glenn T. Seaborg</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white picture of heavy machinery with two operators sitting aside" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2409" data-file-width="3000" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/170px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/255px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg/340px-Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_60-inch_cyclotron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 60-inch cyclotron at the Lawrence Radiation Laboratory, University of California, Berkeley, in August 1939</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg"><img alt="The Seal of the University of California, Berkeley (UC Berkeley)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg/170px-The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg/255px-The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg/340px-The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_University_of_California_Berkeley_1868.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Berkelium is named after UC Berkeley</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although very small amounts of berkelium were possibly produced in previous nuclear experiments, it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discoveries of the chemical elements">first intentionally synthesized</a>, isolated and identified in December 1949 by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ghiorso" title="Albert Ghiorso">Albert Ghiorso</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Thompson" title="Stanley G. Thompson">Stanley G. Thompson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Street,_Jr." title="Kenneth Street, Jr.">Kenneth Street, Jr.</a>. They used the 60-inch <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>. Similar to the nearly simultaneous discovery of <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> (element 95) and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> (element 96) in 1944, the new elements berkelium and <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a> (element 98) were both produced in 1949–1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-c1-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name choice for element 97 followed the previous tradition of the Californian group to draw an analogy between the newly discovered <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> element positioned above it in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. Previously, americium was named after a continent as its analogue <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a>, and curium honored scientists <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a> as the lanthanide above it, <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>, was named after the explorer of the <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a> <a href="/wiki/Johan_Gadolin" title="Johan Gadolin">Johan Gadolin</a>. Thus the discovery report by the Berkeley group reads: "It is suggested that element 97 be given the name berkelium (symbol Bk) after the city of Berkeley in a manner similar to that used in naming its chemical homologue <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a> (atomic number 65) whose name was derived from the town of <a href="/wiki/Ytterby" title="Ytterby">Ytterby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, where the rare earth minerals were first found."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup> This tradition ended on berkelium, though, as the naming of the next discovered actinide, <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a>, was not related to its lanthanide analogue <a href="/wiki/Dysprosium" title="Dysprosium">dysprosium</a>, but after the discovery place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most difficult steps in the synthesis of berkelium were its separation from the final products and the production of sufficient quantities of americium for the target material. First, americium (<sup>241</sup>Am) <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> solution was coated on a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> foil, the solution was evaporated and the residue converted by annealing to <a href="/wiki/Americium_dioxide" title="Americium dioxide">americium dioxide</a> (AmO<sub>2</sub>). This target was irradiated with 35 MeV <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> for 6 hours in the 60-inch cyclotron at the Lawrence Radiation Laboratory, University of California, Berkeley. The (α,2n) reaction induced by the irradiation yielded the <sup>243</sup>Bk isotope and two free <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathrm{^{241}_{\ 95}Am\ +\ ^{4}_{2}He\ \longrightarrow \ ^{243}_{\ 97}Bk\ +\ 2\ ^{1}_{0}n}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/74e8bdfa74e7995738e51a417e98d5e759efbf74" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:34.303ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>After the irradiation, the coating was dissolved with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and then precipitated as the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> using concentrated aqueous <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydroxide" title="Ammonium hydroxide">ammonia solution</a>. The product was <a href="/wiki/Centrifugation" title="Centrifugation">centrifugated</a> and re-dissolved in nitric acid. To separate berkelium from the unreacted americium, this solution was added to a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfate" title="Ammonium sulfate">ammonium sulfate</a> and heated to convert all the dissolved americium into the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> +6. Unoxidized residual americium was precipitated by the addition of <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> as americium(III) <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">AmF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span></span>). This step yielded a mixture of the accompanying product curium and the expected element 97 in form of trifluorides. The mixture was converted to the corresponding hydroxides by treating it with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, and after centrifugation, was dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Perchloric_acid" title="Perchloric acid">perchloric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png"><img alt="Graphs showing similar elution curves (metal amount vs. drops) for (top vs. bottom) terbium vs. berkelium, gadolinium vs. curium, europium vs. americium" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="1218" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/220px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/330px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png/440px-Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elutionskurven_Tb_Gd_Eu_und_Bk_Cm_Am.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">Chromatographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> curves revealing the similarity between the lanthanides <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a> (Tb), <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a> (Gd), and <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a> (Eu) and their corresponding actinides berkelium (Bk), <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> (Cm), and <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> (Am)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Further separation was carried out in the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a>/<a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a> <a href="/wiki/Buffer_solution" title="Buffer solution">buffer solution</a> in a weakly acidic medium (<a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>≈3.5), using <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a> at elevated temperature. The <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatographic</a> separation behavior was then unknown for the element 97, but was anticipated by analogy with terbium (see elution curves). First results were disappointing as no alpha-particle emission signature could be detected from the elution product. Only the further search for <a href="/wiki/K-alpha" title="K-alpha">characteristic X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internal_conversion" title="Internal conversion">conversion electron</a> signals resulted in the identification of a berkelium isotope. Its <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> was uncertain between 243 and 244 in the initial report,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c1_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-c1-18">[18]</a></sup> but was later established as 243.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E97_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-E97-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis_and_extraction">Synthesis and extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Synthesis and extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation_of_isotopes">Preparation of isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Preparation of isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Berkelium is produced by bombarding lighter actinides <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> (<sup>238</sup>U) or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> (<sup>239</sup>Pu) with <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>. In a more common case of uranium fuel, plutonium is produced first by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> (the so-called (n,γ) reaction or neutron fusion) followed by beta-decay:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>92</mn>
</mtd>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

<mtext>U</mtext>

<mover>
<mo>→</mo>

<p>Plutonium-239 is further irradiated by a source that has a high <a href="/wiki/Neutron_flux" title="Neutron flux">neutron flux</a>, several times higher than a conventional nuclear reactor, such as the 85-megawatt <a href="/wiki/High_Flux_Isotope_Reactor" title="High Flux Isotope Reactor">High Flux Isotope Reactor</a> (HFIR) at the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> in Tennessee, USA. The higher flux promotes fusion reactions involving not one but several neutrons, converting <sup>239</sup>Pu to <sup>244</sup>Cm and then to <sup>249</sup>Cm:</p>

<p>The thus-produced <sup>249</sup>Bk has a long half-life of 330 days and thus can capture another neutron. However, the product, <sup>250</sup>Bk, again has a relatively short half-life of 3.212 hours and thus, does not yield any heavier berkelium isotopes. Instead decays to the californium isotope <sup>250</sup>Cf:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>

</mrow>
</mpadded>
<mspace width="-.125em"></mspace>
<mtext>Cf</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {^{249}_{97}Bk-&gt;[{(n,\gamma )}]_{97}^{250}Bk-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][3.212\ {\ce {h}}]_{98}^{250}Cf}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{249}_{97}Bk-&gt;[{(n,\gamma )}]_{97}^{250}Bk-&gt;[\beta ^{-}][3.212\ {\ce {h}}]_{98}^{250}Cf}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/05c55a653575b00e1ccf28b13cca6c4c8a7427b3" style="vertical-align: -1.879ex; margin-right: -0.153ex; margin-top: -0.296ex; margin-bottom: -0.626ex; width:31.364ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Although <sup>247</sup>Bk is the most stable isotope of berkelium, its production in nuclear reactors is very inefficient due to the long half-life of its potential progenitor curium-247, which does not allow it sufficient time to beta decay before capturing another neutron. Thus, <sup>249</sup>Bk is the most accessible isotope of berkelium, which still, is available only in small quantities (only 0.66 grams have been produced in the US over the period 1967–1983<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198430_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198430-33">[33]</a></sup>) at a high price of the order 185 <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">USD</a> per microgram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The isotope <sup>248</sup>Bk was first obtained in 1956 by bombarding a mixture of curium isotopes with 25 MeV α-particles. Although its direct detection was hindered by strong signal interference with <sup>245</sup>Bk, the existence of a new isotope was proven by the growth of the decay product <sup>248</sup>Cf which had been previously characterized. The half-life of <sup>248</sup>Bk was estimated as 23 ± 5 hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> though later 1965 work gave a half-life in excess of 300 years (which may be due to an isomeric state).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Berkelium-247 was produced during the same year by irradiating <sup>244</sup>Cm with alpha-particles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{array}{l}{\ce {^{244}_{96}Cm-&gt;[{\ce {(\alpha ,n)}}]_{98}^{247}Cf-&gt;[\epsilon ][3.11\ {\ce {h}}]_{97}^{247}Bk}}\\{\ce {^{244}_{96}Cm-&gt;[{\ce {(\alpha ,p)}}]_{97}^{247}Bk}}\end{array}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{array}{l}{\ce {^{244}_{96}Cm-&gt;[{\ce {(\alpha ,n)}}]_{98}^{247}Cf-&gt;[\epsilon ][3.11\ {\ce {h}}]_{97}^{247}Bk}}\\{\ce {^{244}_{96}Cm-&gt;[{\ce {(\alpha ,p)}}]_{97}^{247}Bk}}\end{array}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6536c51f66a9ad2efa5fa5d86bc0979e7d70d29e" style="vertical-align: -4.838ex; margin-top: -0.427ex; width:31.961ex; height:11.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Berkelium-242 was synthesized in 1979 by bombarding <sup>235</sup>U with <sup>11</sup>B, <sup>238</sup>U with <sup>10</sup>B, <sup>232</sup>Th with <sup>14</sup>N or <sup>232</sup>Th with <sup>15</sup>N. It converts by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> to <sup>242</sup>Cm with a half-life of 7.0 ± 1.3 minutes. A search for an initially suspected isotope <sup>241</sup>Bk was then unsuccessful;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <sup>241</sup>Bk has since been synthesized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleonica_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-nucleonica-38">[38]</a></sup></p>

</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation">Separation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Separation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The fact that berkelium readily assumes <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> +4 in solids, and is relatively stable in this state in liquids greatly assists separation of berkelium away from many other actinides. These are inevitably produced in relatively large amounts during the nuclear synthesis and often favor the +3 state. This fact was not yet known in the initial experiments, which used a more complex separation procedure. Various oxidation agents can be applied to the berkelium(III) solutions to convert it to the +4 state, such as <a href="/wiki/Bromate" title="Bromate">bromates</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">BrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br/>
3</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Bismuthate" title="Bismuthate">bismuthates</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">BiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">−<br/>
3</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromate" title="Chromate">chromates</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2−<br/>
4</span></span> and Cr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2−<br/>
7</span>), silver(I) thiolate (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ag<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
8</span></span>), lead(IV) oxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">PbO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span></span>), or photochemical oxidation procedures. Berkelium(IV) is then extracted with <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a>, extraction <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a> or liquid-liquid extraction using HDEHP (bis-(2-ethylhexyl) phosphoric scid), <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tributyl_phosphate" title="Tributyl phosphate">tributyl phosphate</a> or various other reagents. These procedures separate berkelium from most trivalent actinides and <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>, except for the lanthanide <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a> (lanthanides are absent in the irradiation target but are created in various <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> decay chains).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198432_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198432-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>A more detailed procedure adopted at the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> was as follows: the initial mixture of actinides is processed with ion exchange using <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">lithium chloride</a> reagent, then precipitated as <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a>, filtered and dissolved in nitric acid. It is then treated with high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">cation exchange</a> resins, and the berkelium phase is oxidized and extracted using one of the procedures described above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198432_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198432-39">[39]</a></sup> Reduction of the thus-obtained berkelium(IV) to the +3 oxidation state yields a solution, which is nearly free from other actinides (but contains cerium). Berkelium and cerium are then separated with another round of ion-exchange treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198433.E2.80.9334_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198433.E2.80.9334-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulk_metal_preparation">Bulk metal preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bulk metal preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In order to characterize chemical and physical properties of solid berkelium and its compounds, a program was initiated in 1952 at the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Material Testing Reactor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, US. It resulted in preparation of an eight-gram plutonium-239 target and in the first production of macroscopic quantities (0.6 micrograms) of berkelium by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Burris_B._Cunningham&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Burris B. Cunningham (page does not exist)">Burris B. Cunningham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Thompson" title="Stanley G. Thompson">Stanley G. Thompson</a> in 1958, after a continuous reactor irradiation of this target for six years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198430_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198430-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This irradiation method was and still is the only way of producing weighable amounts of the element, and most solid-state studies of berkelium have been conducted on microgram or submicrogram-sized samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198438_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198438-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The world's major irradiation sources are the 85-megawatt High Flux Isotope Reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> in Tennessee, USA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and the SM-2 loop reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Research_Institute_of_Atomic_Reactors" title="Research Institute of Atomic Reactors">Research Institute of Atomic Reactors</a> (NIIAR) in <a href="/wiki/Dimitrovgrad,_Russia" title="Dimitrovgrad, Russia">Dimitrovgrad, Russia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> which are both dedicated to the production of transcurium elements (atomic number greater than 96). These facilities have similar power and flux levels, and are expected to have comparable production capacities for transcurium elements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Es_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Es-45">[45]</a></sup> although the quantities produced at NIIAR are not publicly reported. In a "typical processing campaign" at Oak Ridge, tens of grams of <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> are irradiated to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decigram" title="Decigram">decigram</a> quantities of <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milligram" title="Milligram">milligram</a> quantities of berkelium-249 and <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium" title="Einsteinium">einsteinium</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picogram" title="Picogram">picogram</a> quantities of <a href="/wiki/Fermium" title="Fermium">fermium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971262_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971262-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In total, just over one gram of berkelium-249 has been produced at Oak Ridge since 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first berkelium metal sample weighing 1.7 micrograms was prepared in 1971 by the reduction of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium(III)_fluoride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Berkelium(III) fluoride (page does not exist)">berkelium(III) fluoride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> vapor at 1000 °C; the fluoride was suspended on a tungsten wire above a <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> crucible containing molten lithium. Later, metal samples weighing up to 0.5 milligrams were obtained with this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>Similar results are obtained with berkelium(IV) fluoride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressure2_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-pressure2-11">[11]</a></sup> Berkelium metal can also be produced by the reduction of berkelium(IV) oxide with <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_berkelium" title="Compounds of berkelium">Compounds of berkelium</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides">Oxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Oxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two oxides of berkelium are known, with the berkelium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> state of +3 (Bk<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) and +4 (BkO<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Berkelium(IV) oxide is a brown solid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BK_OX_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-BK_OX-51">[51]</a></sup> while berkelium(III) oxide is a yellow-green solid with a melting point of 1920 °C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071972_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071972-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BK_OX_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-BK_OX-51">[51]</a></sup> and is formed from BkO<sub>2</sub> by <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> with molecular <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathrm{2\ BkO_2\ +\ H_2\ \longrightarrow \ Bk_2O_3\ +\ H_2O}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe419b9f853427fc1c60327dfef62ac44822ff86" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:36.104ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Upon heating to 1200 °C, the oxide Bk<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> undergoes a phase change; it undergoes another phase change at 1750 °C. Such three-phase behavior is typical for the actinide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesquioxides" title="Sesquioxides">sesquioxides</a>. Berkelium(II) oxide, BkO, has been reported as a brittle gray solid but its exact chemical composition remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198451_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198451-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>, berkelium assumes the oxidation states +3 and +4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071969_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071969-54">[54]</a></sup> The +3 state is the most stable, especially in solutions, while the tetravalent halides BkF<sub>4</sub> and Cs<sub>2</sub>BkCl<sub>6</sub> are only known in the solid phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447-55">[55]</a></sup> The coordination of berkelium atom in its trivalent fluoride and chloride is tricapped <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry#Trigonal_prismatic_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">trigonal prismatic</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of 9. In trivalent bromide, it is bicapped trigonal prismatic (coordination 8) or <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral</a> (coordination 6),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conv_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-conv-56">[56]</a></sup> and in the iodide it is octahedral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; text-align: center; text-size:90%">
<tr>
<th>Oxidation<br/>
number</th>
<th>F</th>
<th>Cl</th>
<th>Br</th>
<th>I</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>+4</th>
<td>BkF<sub>4</sub><br/>
(yellow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup>)</td>

(orange<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198451_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198451-53">[53]</a></sup>)</td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>+3</th>
<td>BkF<sub>3</sub><br/>
(yellow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup>)</td>
<td>BkCl<sub>3</sub><br/>
(green<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup>)<br/>
Cs<sub>2</sub>NaBkCl<sub>6</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448-58">[58]</a></sup></td>
<td>BkBr<sub>3</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conv_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-conv-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><br/>
(yellow-green<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup>)</td>
<td>BkI<sub>3</sub><br/>
(yellow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270_57-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood19971270-57">[57]</a></sup>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Berkelium(IV) fluoride (BkF<sub>4</sub>) is a yellow-green ionic solid and is isotypic with <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride">uranium tetrafluoride</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirconium(IV)_fluoride" title="Zirconium(IV) fluoride">zirconium(IV) fluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BKF_3_4_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-BKF_3_4-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-f1_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-f1-61">[61]</a></sup> Berkelium(III) fluoride (BkF<sub>3</sub>) is also a yellow-green solid, but it has two crystalline structures. The most stable phase at low temperatures is isotypic with <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_fluoride" title="Yttrium(III) fluoride">yttrium(III) fluoride</a>, while upon heating to between 350 and 600 °C, it transforms to the structure found in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lanthanum(III)_fluoride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lanthanum(III) fluoride (page does not exist)">lanthanum(III) fluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198448-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BKF_3_4_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-BKF_3_4-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Visible amounts of berkelium(III) chloride (BkCl<sub>3</sub>) were first isolated and characterized in 1962, and weighed only 3 billionths of a <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a>. It can be prepared by introducing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> vapors into an evacuated quartz tube containing berkelium oxide at a temperature about 500 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-o1-63">[63]</a></sup> This green solid has a melting point of 600 °C,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071969_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolleman20071969-54">[54]</a></sup> and is isotypic with <a href="/wiki/Uranium(III)_chloride" title="Uranium(III) chloride">uranium(III) chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Upon heating to nearly melting point, BkCl<sub>3</sub> converts into an orthorhombic phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198452_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198452-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two forms of berkelium(III) bromide are known: one with berkelium having coordination 6, and one with coordination 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198438_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198438-42">[42]</a></sup> The latter is less stable and transforms to the former phase upon heating to about 350 °C. An important phenomenon for radioactive solids has been studied on these two crystal forms: the structure of fresh and aged <sup>249</sup>BkBr<sub>3</sub> samples was probed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> over a period longer than 3 years, so that various fractions of berkelium-249 had <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decayed</a> to californium-249. No change in structure was observed upon the <sup>249</sup>BkBr<sub>3</sub>—<sup>249</sup>CfBr<sub>3</sub> transformation. However, other differences were noted for <sup>249</sup>BkBr<sub>3</sub> and <sup>249</sup>CfBr<sub>3</sub>. For example, the latter could be reduced with hydrogen to <sup>249</sup>CfBr<sub>2</sub>, but the former could not – this result was reproduced on individual <sup>249</sup>BkBr<sub>3</sub> and <sup>249</sup>CfBr<sub>3</sub> samples, as well on the samples containing both bromides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conv_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-conv-56">[56]</a></sup> The intergrowth of californium in berkelium occurs at a rate of 0.22% per day and is an intrinsic obstacle in studying berkelium properties. Beside a chemical contamination, <sup>249</sup>Cf, being an alpha emitter, brings undesirable self-damage of the crystal lattice and the resulting self-heating. The chemical effect however can be avoided by performing measurements as a function of time and extrapolating the obtained results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic_compounds">Other inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pnictide" title="Pnictide">pnictides</a> of berkelium-249 of the type BkX are known for the elements <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n1_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-n1-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>. They crystallize in the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">rock-salt structure</a> and are prepared by the reaction of either berkelium(III) hydride (BkH<sub>3</sub>) or metallic berkelium with these elements at elevated temperature (about 600 °C) under high vacuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Berkelium(III) sulfide, Bk<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, is prepared by either treating berkelium oxide with a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> vapors at 1130 °C, or by directly reacting metallic berkelium with elemental sulfur. These procedures yield brownish-black crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198453_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198453-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Berkelium(III) and berkelium(IV) hydroxides are both stable in 1 <a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">molar</a> solutions of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>. Berkelium(III) <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> (BkPO<sub>4</sub>) has been prepared as a solid, which shows strong <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a> under excitation with a green light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198439.E2.80.9340_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198439.E2.80.9340-70">[70]</a></sup> Berkelium hydrides are produced by reacting metal with hydrogen gas at temperatures about 250 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n1_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-n1-67">[67]</a></sup> They are non-stoichiometric with the nominal formula BkH<sub>2+x</sub> (0 &lt; x &lt; 1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198453_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198453-69">[69]</a></sup> Several other salts of berkelium are known, including an oxysulfide (Bk<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>S), and hydrated <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Bk(NO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>·4H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O</span>), chloride (<span class="chemf nowrap">BkCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>·6H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O</span>), <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Bk<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>·12H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O</span>) and <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Bk<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>·4H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198447-55">[55]</a></sup> Thermal decomposition at about 600 °C in an <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> atmosphere (to avoid oxidation to <span class="chemf nowrap">BkO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span>) of <span class="chemf nowrap">Bk<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
3</span>·12H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O</span> yields the crystals of berkelium(III) oxysulfate (<span class="chemf nowrap">Bk<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
4</span></span>). This compound is thermally stable to at least 1000 °C in inert atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198454_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198454-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoberkelium_compounds">Organoberkelium compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Organoberkelium compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Berkelium forms a trigonal (η<sup>5</sup>–C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Bk <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">metallocene</a> complex with three <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl_complex" title="Cyclopentadienyl complex">cyclopentadienyl</a> rings, which can be synthesized by reacting berkelium(III) chloride with the molten beryllocene (<a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">Be</a>(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>) at about 70 °C. It has an amber color and a density of 2.47 g/cm<sup>3</sup>. The complex is stable to heating to at least 250 °C, and sublimates without melting at about 350 °C. The high radioactivity of berkelium gradually destroys the compound (within a period of weeks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-o1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> One cyclopentadienyl ring in (η<sup>5</sup>–C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Bk can be substituted by chlorine to yield [Bk(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Cl]<sub>2</sub>. The optical absorption spectra of this compound are very similar to those of (η<sup>5</sup>–C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Bk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198441-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeterson198454_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeterson198454-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berkelium.jpg"><img alt="A very small sample of a blue liquid in a plastic pipette held by a hand wearing heavy protection equipment" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="487" data-file-width="526" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Berkelium.jpg/220px-Berkelium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Berkelium.jpg/330px-Berkelium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Berkelium.jpg/440px-Berkelium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berkelium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The berkelium target used for the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">tennessine</a> (in dissolved state)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is currently no use for any isotope of berkelium outside of basic scientific research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup> Berkelium-249 is a common target nuclide to prepare still heavier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_elements" title="Transuranic elements">transuranic elements</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transactinides" title="Transactinides">transactinides</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lawrencium" title="Lawrencium">lawrencium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rutherfordium" title="Rutherfordium">rutherfordium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bohrium" title="Bohrium">bohrium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup> It is also useful as a source of the isotope californium-249, which is used for studies on the chemistry of <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a> in preference to the more radioactive californium-252 that is produced in neutron bombardment facilities such as the HFIR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26P_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-H.26P-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 22 milligram batch of berkelium-249 was prepared in a 250-day irradiation and then purified for 90 days at Oak Ridge in 2009. This target yielded the first 6 atoms of <a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">tennessine</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Joint Institute for Nuclear Research</a> (JINR), <a href="/wiki/Dubna" title="Dubna">Dubna</a>, Russia, after bombarding it with calcium ions in the U400 cyclotron for 150 days. This synthesis was a culmination of the Russia—US collaboration between JINR and <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> on the synthesis of elements 113 to 118 which was initiated in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fuel_cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nuclear fuel cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> properties of berkelium are different from those of the neighboring actinides curium and californium, and they suggest berkelium to perform poorly as a fuel in a nuclear reactor. Specifically, berkelium-249 has a moderately large neutron capture <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_cross-section" title="Neutron cross-section">cross section</a> of 710 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">barns</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_neutrons" title="Thermal neutrons">thermal neutrons</a>, 1200 barns <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonance_integral" title="Resonance integral">resonance integral</a>, but very low fission cross section for thermal neutrons. In a thermal reactor, much of it will therefore be converted to berkelium-250 which quickly decays to californium-250.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In principle, berkelium-249 can sustain a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_breeder_reactor" title="Fast breeder reactor">fast breeder reactor</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a> is relatively high at 192 kg; it can be reduced with a water or steel reflector but would still exceed the world production of this isotope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irsn_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-irsn-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Berkelium-247 can maintain chain reaction both in a thermal-neutron and in a fast-neutron reactor, however, its production is rather complex and thus the availability is much lower than its critical mass, which is about 75.7 kg for a bare sphere, 41.2 kg with a water reflector and 35.2 kg with a steel reflector (30 cm thickness).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irsn_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-irsn-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_issues">Health issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berkelium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Health issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Little is known about the effects of berkelium on human body, and analogies with other elements may not be drawn because of different radiation products (<a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> for berkelium and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, or both for most other actinides). The low energy of electrons emitted from berkelium-249 (less than 126 keV) hinders its detection, due to signal interference with other decay processes, but also makes this isotope relatively harmless to humans as compared to other actinides. However, berkelium-249 transforms with a half-life of only 330 days to the strong alpha-emitter californium-249, which is rather dangerous and has to be handled in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glove_box" title="Glove box">glove box</a> in a dedicated laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most available berkelium toxicity data originate from research on animals. Upon ingestion by rats, only about 0.01% berkelium ends in the blood stream. From there, about 65% goes to the bones, where it remains for about 50 years, 25% to the lungs (biological half-life about 20 years), 0.035% to the testicles or 0.01% to the ovaries where berkelium stays indefinitely. The balance of about 10% is excreted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In all these organs berkelium might promote cancer, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal_system" title="Skeletal system">skeletal system</a> its radiation can damage red blood cells. The maximum permissible amount of berkelium-249 in the human skeleton is 0.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanogram" title="Nanogram">nanograms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
